We need to cover all these with 4 lines. Suppose we use two slope -1 lines: y = -x + c1 and y = -x + c2. Each such line covers points with a+b = c. So if we choose c1=4 and c2=5, then these lines cover s=4 and s=5 points: total 3+4=7 points. The remaining points: s=2 and s=3: (1,1),(1,2),(2,1). That's 3 points. We need to cover these with two sunny lines. Can we cover these three points with two sunny lines? As argued, any line through two of these points is non-sunny. So we need to cover each point with a sunny line, but we have two sunny lines, so we can cover at most two points if each sunny line covers at most one point? Actually a sunny line can cover more than one point, but it cannot cover two of these points because that would be non-sunny. So each sunny line can cover at most one of these three points. So with two sunny lines, we can cover at most two points, leaving one uncovered. So impossible.

What if we choose c1=3 and c2=5? Then c=3 covers (1,2),(2,1); c=5 covers (1,4),(2,3),(3,2),(4,1). That's 2+4=6 points. Remaining: s=2: (1,1); s=4: (1,3),(2,2),(3,1). That's 4 points. Need to cover these 4 with two sunny lines. Could we cover all 4 with two sunny lines? Possibly one sunny line covers two of them, and the other sunny line covers the other two. Let's see possible sunny lines through pairs of these points. Points: (1,1),(1,3),(2,2),(3,1). As before, only sunny pair is (1,1)-(2,2) slope1. So one sunny line could cover (1,1) and (2,2). Then we have (1,3) and (3,1) left. Can we cover them with one sunny line? A line through (1,3) and (3,1) has slope -1, non-sunny. So no. Could we have a sunny line that covers (1,3) and (2,2)? That's slope -1, non-sunny. (1,3)-(3,1) slope -1. So no sunny line covers both (1,3) and (3,1). Could we have a sunny line that covers (1,1) and (1,3)? That's vertical, non-sunny. (1,1)-(3,1) horizontal, non-sunny. So the only sunny line that can cover two of these points is the slope1 line through (1,1) and (2,2). So we can cover at most two points with one sunny line. Then we have two points left. Could we cover them with the other sunny line? A sunny line can cover at most one of them if it cannot cover both. So we would need two sunny lines to cover the remaining two points, but we only have one sunny line left. So impossible. What if we choose c1=4 and c2=5? Already did. c1=2 and c2=5? c=2 covers (1,1); c=5 covers 4 points; remaining: s=3,4: (1,2),(2,1),(1,3),(2,2),(3,1) that's 5 points. Need two sunny lines to cover 5 points. Possibly each sunny line covers at most 2 points? But we need to see if there exist sunny lines covering pairs. Points: (1,2),(2,1),(1,3),(2,2),(3,1). Check lines: (1,2)-(2,1) slope -1, non-sunny; (1,2)-(1,3) vertical, non-sunny; (1,2)-(2,2) horizontal, non-sunny; (1,2)-(3,1) slope (1-2)/(3-1) = -1/2, not -1,0,∞ so sunny? Slope -1/2 is not 0,∞,-1, so it's sunny. So line through (1,2) and (3,1) has slope -1/2, sunny. Does it also pass through (2,? )? For x=2, y = -1/2*(2-1)+2 = -0.5+2=1.5, not integer. So it covers only those two points. Another line: (2,1)-(1,3) slope (3-1)/(1-2)=2/(-1)=-2, sunny. So (2,1)-(1,3) slope -2, sunny. (2,1)-(2,2) vertical, non-sunny; (2,1)-(3,1) horizontal, non-sunny; (2,2)-(3,1) slope (1-2)/(3-2)=-1, non-sunny; (1,3)-(2,2) slope -1, non-sunny; (1,3)-(3,1) slope -1, non-sunny; (2,2)-(3,1) slope -1; (1,2)-(2,2) horizontal; etc. So we have some sunny pairs: (1,2)-(3,1) and (2,1)-(1,3). Also (1,2)-(2,1) is non-sunny; (1,3)-(2,2) non-sunny; (2,2)-(3,1) non-sunny; (1,2)-(1,3) vertical; (2,1)-(2,2) vertical; (2,1)-(3,1) horizontal; (1,3)-(3,1) slope -1; etc. So we could try to cover the 5 points with two sunny lines: one line covers (1,2) and (3,1); another line covers (2,1) and (1,3). That covers 4 points. The remaining point (2,2) is uncovered. Could we cover (2,2) with one of these lines? The first line through (1,2) and (3,1) does not contain (2,2) because for x=2, y=1.5. The second line through (2,1) and (1,3) does not contain (2,2) because for x=2, y=1? Actually (2,1) is on it, (1,3) gives slope -2, line: y-1=-2(x-2) => y=-2x+5. For x=2, y=1; for x=1, y=3; for x=2.5, y=0? Not (2,2). So (2,2) is not on either. So we need a third sunny line to cover (2,2), but we only have two sunny lines. Could we cover (2,2) with a non-sunny line? But we already used two non-sunny lines (c=2 and c=5). Could we change the non-sunny lines to include (2,2)? For instance, use c=4 and c=5? That gave uncovered (1,1),(1,2),(2,1). Then we have two sunny lines to cover those three. As before, impossible. What if we use c=3 and c=4? Then c=3 covers (1,2),(2,1); c=4 covers (1,3),(2,2),(3,1). That's 2+3=5 points. Remaining: s=2: (1,1); s=5: (1,4),(2,3),(3,2),(4,1) that's 4 points. Total 9 points? Actually we have 10 points, so 5 covered, 5 uncovered: (1,1),(1,4),(2,3),(3,2),(4,1). Need two sunny lines to cover these 5. Could we cover all 5 with two sunny lines? Possibly one sunny line covers two, another covers two, and one point left? But we need to cover all. Let's see points: (1,1),(1,4),(2,3),(3,2),(4,1). Check lines: (1,1)-(2,3) slope 2, sunny; (1,1)-(3,2) slope 1/2, sunny; (1,1)-(4,1) horizontal, non-sunny; (1,4)-(2,3) slope -1, non-sunny; (1,4)-(3,2) slope -1, non-sunny; (1,4)-(4,1) slope (1-4)/(4-1) = -3/3 = -1, non-sunny; (2,3)-(3,2) slope -1, non-sunny; (2,3)-(4,1) slope (1-3)/(4-2) = -2/2 = -1, non-sunny; (3,2)-(4,1) slope -1, non-sunny. What about using c=2 and c=4? Then c=2 covers (1,1); c=4 covers (1,3),(2,2),(3,1). That's 1+3=4 points. Remaining: s=3: (1,2),(2,1); s=5: (1,4),(2,3),(3,2),(4,1) that's 2+4=6 points. Need two sunny lines to cover 6 points. Possibly each sunny line covers at most 2 points? But we need to cover 6 points with two lines, so each must cover at least 3 points. Can a sunny line cover 3 points among these? Points: (1,2),(2,1),(1,4),(2,3),(3,2),(4,1). Check if any line can contain three of them. For a line to contain three lattice points, they must be collinear. Could there be a line through (1,2),(2,1),(3,0) not in set; (1,2),(2,3),(3,4) maybe? (1,2) to (2,3) slope1, line y=x+1. Does it contain (3,4)? 3+1=4 yes, but (3,4) not in set. (1,4),(2,3),(3,2) slope -1, non-sunny. (1,4),(2,3),(3,2) is slope -1, not sunny. (2,1),(3,2),(4,3) slope1, but (4,3) not in set. (1,2),(3,2) horizontal y=2, non-sunny. (2,1),(4,1) horizontal y=1, non-sunny. So no sunny line contains three of these points. So each sunny line can cover at most 2 points. Then two lines cover at most 4 points, leaving at least 2 uncovered. So impossible.

Thus for n=4, k=2 seems impossible. But we haven't exhausted all possibilities for non-sunny lines: they could be a mix of vertical, horizontal, slope -1. Also we could have more than two non-sunny lines? For k=2, we have exactly 2 sunny lines and 2 non-sunny lines. So we need to choose 2 non-sunny lines (could be both vertical, both horizontal, both slope -1, or one vertical and one horizontal, etc.) and 2 sunny lines covering all points. Let's try to see if there is any configuration that works.

We can try to use a vertical line x=1 and a horizontal line y=1. Then covered points: all with x=1 or y=1. That's 4+4-1=7 points (since (1,1) counted twice). The uncovered points: those with x≥2 and y≥2: (2,2),(2,3),(3,2),(4,1)? Actually (4,1) has y=1 so covered; (3,1) covered; (2,1) covered; (1,4) covered; (1,3) covered; (1,2) covered; (1,1) covered. Thus k=2 seems impossible for n=4.

What about k=3? That would be 3 sunny lines and 1 non-sunny line. Can we cover all 10 points with 1 non-sunny line and 3 sunny lines? Possibly. Let's try to see if we can find such a configuration.

We need to cover all points. The non-sunny line could be vertical, horizontal, or slope -1. Suppose we take vertical line x=1. Then points with x=1 are covered: (1,1),(1,2),(1,3),(1,4). (2,1)-(2,2): vertical x=2, non-sunny.

Thus for n=4, possible k: 0,1,3? We also have k=2? We didn't find a configuration. Could k=4? All sunny lines. For n=4, we need 4 sunny lines covering all 10 points. Is that possible? Possibly. Thus pattern: k can be 0,1,3? But n=3 gave k=0,1. n=4 gave 0,1,3. n=5? Possibly k=0,1,3,5? Or maybe k can be any integer from 0 to n except 2? Let's test n=5. Points: a,b≥1, a+b ≤6. So points: s=2: (1,1); s=3: (1,2),(2,1); s=4: (1,3),(2,2),(3,1); s=5: (1,4),(2,3),(3,2),(4,1); s=6: (1,5),(2,4),(3,3),(4,2),(5,1). Total points = 1+2+3+4+5=15. Need 5 lines covering all 15 points.

We already have vertical lines x=1,...,5 covering all points, so k=0 works. Horizontal y=1,...,5 works. k=0.

Can we have k=1? Use 4 non-sunny lines and 1 sunny line. For n=5, we can take vertical lines x=1,2,3,4? That would cover points with x=1..4. Then points with x=5: (5,1) only (since a=5,b≤1). So uncovered (5,1). Then one sunny line through (5,1) works. So k=1 possible.

Can we have k=2? That would be 2 sunny lines and 3 non-sunny lines. Need to cover all 15 points with 3 non-sunny lines and 2 sunny lines. Is that possible? Possibly. Let's try to see if we can design a configuration.

We need to cover the triangular lattice points. The non-sunny lines can be vertical, horizontal, or slope -1. What about 3 slope -1 lines? For n=5, points with sums: s=2,3,4,5,6. Slope -1 lines y=-x+c cover points with a+b=c. So we could choose c=4,5,6. What about mixing: e.g., 2 vertical and 1 horizontal? That would be 3 non-sunny lines. Let's try to see if we can cover all points with 2 vertical lines and 1 horizontal line, and then 2 sunny lines. So conjecture: k=2 is impossible for all n≥3. But we need to check n=3,4,5,6. So far yes. Could there be a configuration with k=2 for larger n? The remaining points (N - n) must be covered by non-sunny lines. But non-sunny lines are of types that can cover many points: e.g., a vertical line x=i covers points with that x, which is about n-i+1 points; a horizontal line y=j covers about n-j+1; a slope -1 line y=-x+c covers points with sum=c, which is about c-1 points. So if we have n-k non-sunny lines, they can cover at most the sum of the largest n-k such lines. But we need to cover N - (points covered by sunny lines) points. For k=2, we have n-2 non-sunny lines. The maximum total points they can cover is when we choose the n-2 lines that cover the most points. But we also need to ensure that the sunny lines cover points that are not covered by non-sunny lines, otherwise we might have overlap. However, we can choose the lines to minimize overlap. So we need to see if there exists a set of n lines (2 sunny, n-2 non-sunny) covering all points. This is a covering problem.

We can try to prove that k=2 is impossible. Perhaps we can use a counting argument: each point (a,b) is on at least one line. The lines are distinct. For each line, we can consider the number of points it contains. We need to sum over lines of the number of points on them, but points can be on multiple lines, so the sum is at least the number of points. We can try to get an upper bound on the sum of points per line given the types of lines. For sunny lines, the maximum number of points they can contain is limited. If we can show that for any configuration with 2 sunny lines, the sum of points on lines is less than N, then it's impossible. But we need to consider the worst-case for the sum. Actually we want to prove that no configuration exists. So we need to show that for any set of n lines with exactly 2 sunny lines, the total number of distinct points covered is less than N. But we can choose the lines to maximize coverage. So we need to find the maximum possible number of distinct points covered by n lines with exactly 2 sunny lines. If that maximum is less than N, then k=2 impossible. So we need to find the maximum coverage.

Now, what about k=2? We need to prove impossible. Let's attempt a proof.

We have n lines. For each point (a,b) with a,b≥1, a+b≤n+1, it lies on at least one line. Let L be the set of lines. Define for each line ℓ, let S(ℓ) be the set of points on ℓ. We need to show that if exactly 2 lines are sunny, then the total number of distinct points covered is less than N. Alternatively, we can use a combinatorial argument: each point (a,b) can be assigned to a line that contains it. We can try to show that the number of points that can be covered by 2 sunny lines is limited, and the remaining points cannot be covered by n-2 non-sunny lines because non-sunny lines are of types that can cover many points, but we need to cover all points. Perhaps we can use a counting of "incidences" with some weighting.

Another approach: Consider the set of points. For each point (a,b), define its "type" maybe based on the sum a+b. The points are in triangular array. The lines are either horizontal, vertical, or slope -1, or other slopes. For non-sunny lines, they are of types that are "axis-aligned" or slope -1. For sunny lines, they are not. We can try to show that any set of n lines that covers all points must contain at least 3 sunny lines, except possibly when n=3? But we have n=3 with k=1 possible. So for n≥4, k must be at least 3? But we have k=0,1 possible. So that's not true. So we need to show that k=2 is impossible, but k=0,1,3 are possible.

Let's try to prove that k=2 is impossible for n≥3. Suppose we have exactly 2 sunny lines. Then the other n-2 lines are non-sunny: they are either horizontal, vertical, or slope -1. We need to cover all points. Consider the set of points with a+b = n+1 (the largest sum). There are n points: (1,n),(2,n-1),...,(n,1). These points are in the triangle. They must be covered by some lines. For a line to cover a point with a+b = n+1, it must be either a horizontal line y = b (for some b), a vertical line x = a, or a slope -1 line y = -x + c (with c = n+1). But note that horizontal lines y = b for b from 1 to n, vertical lines x = a for a from 1 to n, and slope -1 lines y = -x + c for c from 2 to n+1. So the points on the boundary can be covered by these lines. If we have only 2 sunny lines, they might also cover some of these points, but sunny lines cannot be horizontal, vertical, or slope -1, so they cannot cover any point on the boundary? Actually they can cover points on the boundary, because a point (a,b) with a+b=n+1 can lie on a line that is not horizontal, vertical, or slope -1. For example, the line y = 2x -1 passes through (1,1) which is not on the boundary for n>2? For n=5, boundary points: (1,5),(2,4),(3,3),(4,2),(5,1). The line y=2x-1 passes through (1,1) not boundary; (2,3) is on boundary? (2,3) sum5, not 6; so not. But a line like y = 3x -2 passes through (1,1) not boundary; (2,4) sum6, yes (2,4) is on boundary for n=5? Actually n=5, a+b≤6, so boundary is sum=6: (1,5),(2,4),(3,3),(4,2),(5,1). So (2,4) is boundary. So a sunny line can contain boundary points. So we need to consider that.

Maybe we can use a parity or modulo argument. Alternatively, we can try to use the fact that the points are in a triangular grid, and the lines are distinct. Perhaps we can use a double counting of the number of points on each line, and show that the sum of points on all lines (counting multiplicities) is at most something, but we need at least N. For non-sunny lines, they can contain many points, but we have only n-2 of them. The maximum total points on n-2 non-sunny lines is when we choose the n-2 lines that cover the most points. For n≥3, the maximum points from n-2 non-sunny lines is achieved by taking the n-2 vertical lines with smallest x? Actually vertical lines x=i cover points with that x. The number of points on vertical line x=i is n-i+1 (since y from 1 to n-i+1). So the sum of points on vertical lines x=1..(n-2) is sum_{i=1}^{n-2} (n-i+1) = sum_{k=3}^{n} k? Actually n-i+1 for i=1..n-2 gives: n, n-1, ..., 3. So sum = (n+3)(n-2)/2? That's about n^2/2. For n=5, sum = 5+4+3=12. The total points N=15. So 12 points from 3 vertical lines, leaving 3 points to be covered by 2 sunny lines. But we already saw that 2 sunny lines cannot cover 3 points because the only possible sunny lines through these points are non-sunny? Actually we need to check if the remaining points can be covered by 2 sunny lines. For n=5, after covering x=1,2,3 (vertical lines), we have uncovered: (4,1),(4,2),(5,1) — 3 points. Can we cover these with 2 sunny lines? We tried and found no sunny line covering two of them. So impossible. So the maximum points from n-2 non-sunny lines is not enough to cover all points if we only have 2 sunny lines. But we could also choose non-sunny lines that are not all vertical; we could mix horizontal and slope -1 lines to cover more points. For example, for n=5, we could take slope -1 lines c=4,5? That covers 3+4=7 points; plus maybe a vertical line x=1 covers 5 points, but overlap. The total distinct points from 3 non-sunny lines could be up to 12? Actually we need to consider the maximum possible distinct points covered by n-2 non-sunny lines, given that we also have 2 sunny lines that can cover points not covered by non-sunny lines. But we need to cover all points. So we need to show that for any set of n-2 non-sunny lines, the number of points they cover is at most something less than N minus the maximum points that 2 sunny lines can cover. But we can also choose the sunny lines to cover points that are not covered by non-sunny lines. So we need to find the maximum possible coverage by n lines with exactly 2 sunny lines. If that maximum is less than N, then k=2 impossible.

We can try to find an upper bound on the number of distinct points that can be covered by n lines with exactly 2 sunny lines. Since we have freedom to choose the lines, we want to maximize coverage. So we need to find the best possible configuration. We can try to argue that the maximum coverage is achieved by taking the non-sunny lines as vertical lines with smallest x, and then the sunny lines as lines that cover the remaining points. But we already saw that for n=5, that gives 12 points from 3 vertical lines, and then 2 sunny lines can cover at most 2 points each (since no triple on sunny line among remaining), so max 4 points, total 16, but we need 15. Actually 12+4=16 >15, so it's possible that we could cover all 15 points with 2 sunny lines? But we need to check if the 2 sunny lines can cover the 3 uncovered points. We have 3 points left, and 2 sunny lines can cover at most 4 points, but we need to cover exactly those 3 points. However, we saw that no sunny line can contain two of those points, so each sunny line can cover at most one of them, so 2 sunny lines cover at most 2 points, leaving one uncovered. So we need to cover 3 points with 2 sunny lines, impossible. So the maximum coverage from 3 vertical lines and 2 sunny lines is 12+2=14, not 15. So we cannot cover all points. Could we choose a different set of non-sunny lines that cover more points? For n=5, the maximum points from 3 non-sunny lines is 12? Could we get 13? For example, take vertical x=1 (5 points), horizontal y=1 (5 points), and slope -1 line y=-x+5 (4 points). Overlap: (1,1) in both x=1 and y=1; (1,4) in x=1 and c=5? Actually (1,4) is on x=1 and c=5? y=-x+5 gives (1,4) yes; (2,3) on y=1? No, y=1 gives (2,1); (2,3) not; (2,3) on c=5? Yes; so overlaps. Total distinct: 5+5+4-2? Actually (1,1) counted twice; (1,4) counted twice; maybe (2,3) not? Let's compute: x=1 covers (1,1)-(1,5); y=1 covers (1,1)-(5,1); c=5 covers (1,4),(2,3),(3,2),(4,1). So distinct = 5+5+4-3=11. So 11 points. That's less than 12. So vertical lines give more. So the maximum distinct points from 3 non-sunny lines is 12 (from x=1,2,3). So we cannot get 13. Now, we need to prove that k=2 is impossible. Let's attempt a rigorous proof.

Assume we have n lines, exactly 2 sunny, and they cover T_n. Let the set of non-sunny lines be N. They are of types: horizontal, vertical, slope -1. We'll show that the union of N cannot cover all points, and the remaining points cannot be covered by 2 sunny lines.

Idea: Consider the points on the boundary: those with a+b = n+1. There are n such points. Each non-sunny line can cover at most 2 of these boundary points, unless it is a slope -1 line that covers all n. But if we have a slope -1 line that covers all boundary points, then it is non-sunny. Then the remaining points (interior) must be covered by the other lines. So we need to consider cases.

Let's try to prove that any covering of T_n with n lines must contain at least 3 sunny lines, except possibly when n=3. But we have k=0,1 possible. So that's not true. So we need to show that k=2 is impossible, but k=0,1,3 are possible. So we need to argue that if exactly 2 lines are sunny, then the total number of distinct points covered is at most N-1. How to show that?

Perhaps we can use a counting argument with "incidences" of points with lines, and use the fact that each sunny line can contain at most floor((n+1)/2) points, and each non-sunny line can contain at most n points, but we have n-2 non-sunny lines. For T_{n-1}, the covering number is n-1. So we need at least n-1 lines to cover T_{n-1}. But we only have n-2 lines. Therefore, it's impossible. This is a rigorous argument.

Thus we have shown that if we have a covering with exactly 2 sunny lines, then either we have no slope -1 line covering all boundary points, which leads to a contradiction via the counting argument (|X|+|Y| = n+2 vs ≤ n), or we have a slope -1 line covering all boundary points, then the remaining lines must cover T_{n-1}, but we have only n-2 lines, which is less than the minimum required (n-1). Therefore, k=2 is impossible for all n≥3. This completes the proof that k=2 is not possible.

Now, we need to also determine which k are possible. We have:
- k=0: always possible (vertical lines x=1,...,n).
- k=1: always possible (take vertical lines x=1,...,n-1 and one sunny line through (n,1) e.g., y=2x-(2n-1)).
- k=3: possible for n≥4. Construction: For n≥5, take vertical lines x=1,...,n-3 (these are n-3 lines) and then three sunny lines as described: L1: y=x-(n-3); L2: y=-(x/2)+(n+2)/2? Actually we need to ensure the lines are sunny. The construction we gave for n=6 works. For general n, we can take the n-3 vertical lines x=1,...,n-3. Then the uncovered points are those with x≥n-2. There are 6 points: (n-2,1),(n-2,2),(n-2,3),(n-1,1),(n-1,2),(n,1). Then we define three sunny lines:
   L1: y = x - (n-3) (passes through (n-2,1) and (n-1,2))
   L2: y = -x/2 + (n+2)/2 (passes through (n-2,2) and (n,1))
   L3: y = -2x + (2n-1) (passes through (n-2,3) and (n-1,1))
Check that these lines are sunny: slopes 1, -1/2, -2, all not 0,∞,-1. So they are sunny. And they cover all 6 points. So total lines: n-3 vertical + 3 sunny = n lines. Exactly 3 sunny lines. This works for n≥5. For n=4, we need a separate construction. For n=4, we can take vertical lines x=1 and x=2 (2 lines) and then three sunny lines covering the remaining 3 points: (3,1),(3,2),(4,1). Now, we need to produce a rigorous solution.

Let's write the solution step by step.

First, define T_n = {(a,b)∈ℕ^2 : a,b≥1, a+b≤n+1}. There are N = n(n+1)/2 points.

We need to find all k such that there exist n distinct lines covering T_n, with exactly k sunny lines.

We'll prove:
- k=0 is always possible.
- k=1 is always possible.
- k=3 is possible for n≥4.
- k=2 is impossible for all n≥3.
- For n=3, k=0,1 are possible; for n≥4, k=0,1,3 are possible.

We need to verify that k=3 is possible for n=4 as well. The construction for n=4: take vertical lines x=1,2 (2 lines) and then three sunny lines: e.g., y=2x-5, y=2x-4, y=2x-7. That covers all points. So k=3 works.

Now, we need to show that k=2 is impossible. We'll do a case analysis.

Assume there exists a set of n lines covering T_n with exactly 2 sunny lines. Let the sunny lines be L1 and L2. Consider the boundary B = {(a,b)∈T_n : a+b=n+1}. There are n points. Each line that is not sunny is either horizontal, vertical, or slope -1. If a line is slope -1, it contains all points with a+b=c for some c. If c=n+1, it contains all boundary points. So we have two cases.

Case 1: There is a non-sunny line ℓ with equation y=-x+(n+1). Then ℓ covers all boundary points. The remaining points are those with a+b≤n, i.e., T_{n-1}. We need to cover T_{n-1} with the other n-2 lines (2 sunny and n-3 non-sunny). But it is known that the minimum number of lines needed to cover T_{n-1} is n-1 (since each line can contain at most n-1 points, and T_{n-1} has (n-1)n/2 points). More rigorously: For any line ℓ, the number of points of T_{n-1} on ℓ is at most n-1 (if ℓ is vertical x=1, it contains n-1 points; if ℓ is slope -1, it contains at most n-1 points; if ℓ is other slope, it contains at most floor((n-1)/2) points). But we need a lower bound on the number of lines required. Since T_{n-1} has (n-1)n/2 points, and each line can cover at most n-1 points, we need at least n lines? Wait, (n-1)n/2 divided by (n-1) = n/2, so that's not a good bound. Actually we need to show that the covering number is at least n-1. The known result: the minimum number of lines needed to cover all points of a triangular array of side m is m. For m=n-1, that's n-1. So we need at least n-1 lines. But we have only n-2 lines. So impossible. This is a standard fact; we can prove it by considering that each line can contain at most 2 points from the set of points with a+b = n (the next layer)? Actually we can give a direct proof: In T_{n-1}, consider the points with a+b = n. There are n-1 such points. Any line that is not slope -1 can contain at most 1 of them? Let's analyze: A line with slope m≠-1 can contain at most 1 point from the set of points with a+b = n? For a line with slope -1, it contains all points with a+b = constant, so it can contain at most 1 such set (if c=n, it contains all n-1 points). So if we have a slope -1 line with c=n, it covers all n-1 points of that layer. So we could cover that layer with one line. Then the next layer a+b = n-1 has n-2 points, etc. So the covering number for T_{n-1} is n-1 if we use slope -1 lines? Actually we can cover T_{n-1} with n-1 slope -1 lines: c=3,...,n+1? But T_{n-1} has sums from 2 to n. So we need c=2,...,n. That's n-1 lines. So the covering number is n-1. So we need at least n-1 lines. Since we have n-2 lines, impossible. So case 1 is impossible.

Case 2: There is no non-sunny line that covers all boundary points. Then each non-sunny line can cover at most 1 boundary point (since horizontal and vertical cover exactly 1, and slope -1 with c≠n+1 covers 0). There are n-2 non-sunny lines, so they can cover at most n-2 boundary points. Since the two sunny lines can cover at most 2 boundary points (each can cover at most 1), the total boundary points covered is at most n, which is exactly n. For a covering, we need all n boundary points to be covered. Therefore, the non-sunny lines must cover exactly n-2 boundary points, and the sunny lines must cover the remaining 2 boundary points. So we have a bijection: each boundary point is either on a sunny line or on a non-sunny line. The two sunny lines cover two distinct boundary points, say B1=(i1, n+1-i1) and B2=(i2, n+1-i2). The other n-2 boundary points are covered by the non-sunny lines. Since each non-sunny line covers exactly one boundary point, they are all either horizontal or vertical. Let V be the set of x-coordinates of vertical non-sunny lines, and H be the set of y-coordinates of horizontal non-sunny lines. Then for each i not in {i1,i2}, we have i∈V or n+1-i∈H. Now, define X = {1,...,n}\V and Y = {1,...,n}\H. Then as argued, for any x∈X, we have x∉V, so the condition forces n+1-x∈H, i.e., n+1-x∉Y. Thus Y∩(n+1-X)=∅. Similarly, X∩(n+1-Y)=∅. The map i→n+1-i is a bijection, so |n+1-X|=|X|, |n+1-Y|=|Y|. Hence |Y| ≤ n - |X| and |X| ≤ n - |Y|. Therefore, |X|+|Y| ≤ n. On the other hand, the number of non-sunny lines is |V|+|H| = n-2. But |V| = n - |X|, |H| = n - |Y|, so (n - |X|)+(n - |Y|)= n-2 ⇒ 2n - (|X|+|Y|)= n-2 ⇒ |X|+|Y| = n+2. This contradicts |X|+|Y| ≤ n. Hence case 2 is impossible.

Thus k=2 is impossible for all n≥3.

Now, we need to show that k=0,1,3 are possible for n≥4. For n=3, we have k=0,1 possible. We'll give explicit constructions.

- For k=0: take lines x=1, x=2, ..., x=n. These are all vertical, hence not sunny. They cover all points because for any (a,b)∈T_n, a∈{1,...,n}, so it lies on line x=a.
- For k=1: take lines x=1, x=2, ..., x=n-1 (these are n-1 vertical lines, not sunny) and one line through the remaining point (n,1) with slope 2, e.g., y-1=2(x-n). This line is sunny because slope 2 ≠0,∞,-1. It covers (n,1) and possibly other points, but that's fine. The lines are distinct. So we have n lines with exactly 1 sunny line.
- For k=3: we need to construct for n≥4. For n=4, take lines x=1, x=2 (2 vertical lines) and three sunny lines: e.g., L1: y=2x-5 (through (3,1)), L2: y=2x-4 (through (3,2)), L3: y=2x-7 (through (4,1)). Check coverage: x=1 covers (1,1)-(1,4); x=2 covers (2,1)-(2,3); L1 covers (3,1); L2 covers (3,2); L3 covers (4,1). All points covered. So k=3 works for n=4. For n≥5, take vertical lines x=1,2,...,n-3 (these are n-3 lines, not sunny). Then the uncovered points are those with x≥n-2. There are 6 such points: (n-2,1),(n-2,2),(n-2,3),(n-1,1),(n-1,2),(n,1). Define three sunny lines:
   L1: y = x - (n-3) (passes through (n-2,1) and (n-1,2))
   L2: y = -x/2 + (n+2)/2 (passes through (n-2,2) and (n,1))
   L3: y = -2x + (2n-1) (passes through (n-2,3) and (n-1,1))
These lines are sunny because slopes are 1, -1/2, -2, none of which are 0,∞,-1. They cover all 6 points. So total lines: n-3 vertical + 3 sunny = n lines, exactly 3 sunny. Thus k=3 is possible for all n≥4.

Now, we also need to check if k=2 is possible for n=3? We already know it's impossible. So the set of possible k for n=3 is {0,1}. For n≥4, it's {0,1,3}.

But we should also consider if k=0,1,3 are possible for n=3? For n=3, k=3 is impossible (we already saw). So answer: For n=3, k=0,1; for n≥4, k=0,1,3.

Thus the solution is complete.

But note that T_{n-2} has (n-2)(n-1)/2 points. The number of lines is n-2, which is less than the number of lines needed for a covering? For m = n-2, the base case says that T_m cannot be covered by m lines with exactly 2 sunny lines. But here we have n-2 lines covering T_{n-2}. That's exactly m lines covering T_m. So by the induction hypothesis, this is impossible. Therefore, subcase 1a is impossible.

- Subcase 1b: No non-sunny line is slope -1 with c=n. Then each non-sunny line can cover at most 1 point from the set of sum=n points. There are n-3 non-sunny lines, so they can cover at most n-3 points. But there are n-1 points to cover. The two sunny lines can cover at most 2 points (each at most 1). So total points covered ≤ n-1. For a covering, we need all n-1 points covered. Therefore, we need n-1 ≤ (n-3)+2 = n-1, so equality holds. This forces that the n-3 non-sunny lines cover exactly n-3 points, and the two sunny lines cover the remaining 2 points. So we have a bijection: each point of sum=n is either on a sunny line or on a non-sunny line. The two sunny lines each contain exactly one point of sum=n. The non-sunny lines each contain exactly one point of sum=n. So we have a set of n-1 lines (2 sunny + n-3 non-sunny) covering these n-1 points, each line covering exactly one. Now, consider the points of sum=n-1 in T_{n-1}. There are n-2 such points. They must be covered by the lines. The sunny lines already contain one point each from sum=n. They could also contain points from sum=n-1. But note that any line through two points of sum=n-1 has slope -1, which is not sunny. So the sunny lines can contain at most 1 point from each sum layer. So they can contain at most 2 points from sum=n-1 (one each). The non-sunny lines can contain points from sum=n-1. Among the non-sunny lines, we have n-3 lines, each covering one point from sum=n. They could also cover points from sum=n-1. But we need to cover all n-2 points of sum=n-1. If we have a non-sunny line that is slope -1 with c=n-1, it can cover all n-2 points. So we need to consider the structure.

Now, we need a general proof. Perhaps we can use an invariant like the sum of the coordinates of all points on each line, but we need to show that the total sum of coordinates over all points (counting multiplicities) is less than something. Another idea: Use a "potential" function that is the sum of the squares of the coordinates. For a line ℓ, the sum of squares of points on ℓ might be bounded. Wait, we need to prove that k=2 is impossible for all n≥3. The proof we gave for subcase B is correct. For subcase A, we need to show that it's also impossible. Perhaps we can use an inductive argument that reduces n by 2, as we attempted. Let's try to prove by induction on n that no covering of T_n by n lines can have exactly 2 sunny lines.

Base case n=3: we already argued it's impossible. For n=4, we can also argue it's impossible. But we need a general induction.

Induction hypothesis: For all m < n, there is no covering of T_m by m lines with exactly 2 sunny lines.

Now, consider a covering of T_n by n lines with exactly 2 sunny lines. We have two cases.

Case 1: There is a non-sunny line ℓ with equation y=-x+(n+1). Then ℓ covers all boundary points. The remaining lines are 2 sunny lines L1, L2 and n-3 other non-sunny lines. They must cover T_{n-1}. Now, consider the points with sum = n in T_{n-1}. There are n-1 such points. They cannot be covered by L1 or L2 (since any line through two such points has slope -1). Therefore, they must be covered by the other n-3 non-sunny lines. If any of these non-sunny lines is slope -1 with c=n, it covers all n-1 points. Then we have a covering of T_{n-2} by n-2 lines (2 sunny + n-4 other non-sunny) with exactly 2 sunny lines. By induction, this is impossible. So we must have that no non-sunny line is slope -1 with c=n. Then each non-sunny line can cover at most 1 point from the set of sum=n points. There are n-3 non-sunny lines, so they cover at most n-3 points. The two sunny lines cover at most 2 points (each at most 1). So total points covered ≤ n-1. But we need to cover all n-1 points. Therefore, equality must hold: the n-3 non-sunny lines cover exactly n-3 points, and the two sunny lines cover the remaining 2 points. So each point of sum=n is on exactly one line. Now, consider the points with sum = n-1 in T_{n-1}. There are n-2 such points. They must be covered by the lines. The sunny lines already contain one point each from sum=n. They could also contain points from sum=n-1. The non-sunny lines each contain one point from sum=n, and they could also contain points from sum=n-1. We need to cover all n-2 points of sum=n-1. If any non-sunny line is slope -1 with c=n-1, it can cover all n-2 points. So we need to consider the structure. This is getting into a combinatorial analysis of the "covering" of layers.

Perhaps we can use a "double counting" of the number of points on each line, and use the fact that each line can contain at most 2 points from the set of points with a+b = n+1? Actually we already used that. For the layers, we can consider the "center of mass" of the points on each line. Another approach: Use the concept of "Sperner families" or "antichains". Define for each point (a,b) the value f(a,b) = a + b. Then the sum of f over all points in T_n is S = sum_{s=2}^{n+1} (s-1)s = sum_{s=2}^{n+1} (s^2 - s) = sum_{s=2}^{n+1} s^2 - sum_{s=2}^{n+1} s. That's something. But we need to relate to lines.

Maybe we can use a weighting that gives more weight to points with small sum. For example, w(a,b) = 1/(a+b). Then the total weight is about n - ln(n). Let's try to formalize.

We have n lines total. Among them, exactly 2 are sunny. The other n-2 are non-sunny. They can be of three types: horizontal, vertical, slope -1. We need to cover all points of T_n. Since we have only n lines total, and we need to cover many layers, the number of slope -1 lines we can have is limited. The non-sunny lines that are not slope -1 can cover at most 1 point per layer. But we need to cover all points. The total number of points in T_n is N = n(n+1)/2. The total number of points that can be covered by the lines if each line covers at most 1 point per layer is at most (n) * (n) = n^2? That's not helpful.

We need a more precise counting. Let's consider the layers from top to bottom. Let L_s be the set of points with a+b=s. There are s-1 points. The lines that can cover these points are: slope -1 lines with constant s, or other lines. If we have a slope -1 line with constant s, it covers all s-1 points. So we want to use slope -1 lines to cover as many points as possible. Since we have only n-2 non-sunny lines, and we need to cover all points, the maximum number of slope -1 lines we can have is n-2 (if all non-sunny lines are slope -1). But then we would have only 2 sunny lines, which are not slope -1. So the total number of slope -1 lines is at most n-2. To cover all layers, we need to cover layers s=2,...,n+1. If we use a slope -1 line for each layer, we would need n slope -1 lines (since there are n layers). But we have at most n-2 slope -1 lines. Therefore, at least 2 layers must be covered by other lines. Those layers have many points. For a layer s, if we do not have a slope -1 line with constant s, then each point in that layer must be covered by a line that is not slope -1. Since each such line can cover at most 1 point from that layer, we need at least (s-1) lines to cover that layer. But we have only n lines total. So the layers that are not covered by slope -1 lines must be small. For example, if we have a layer s with s-1 points, and we cover it by non-slope -1 lines, we need at least s-1 lines. Since we have only n lines, this forces s-1 ≤ n. That's always true for s≤n+1. But we also have to cover other layers. So the total number of lines needed to cover all layers if we use slope -1 lines for some layers and individual points for others is at least the sum over layers of (number of points in layer) divided by (max points per line for that layer). But we have a limited number of lines.

Given the time, I think we can produce a rigorous proof by considering the "sum of the coordinates" of the points on each line, and using a double counting argument that leads to a contradiction. Maybe we can use a function that is "convex" in a way that the sum on a line is at most something, and the total sum over all points is larger than n times that something. Maybe we can use a function that is "increasing" in the sum, so that points with small sum have larger weight. For example, w(a,b) = (a+b). Then the total sum is n(n+1)(n+2)/3. Given the difficulty, perhaps we can use a "potential" function that is the sum of the squares of the coordinates. For a point (a,b), define p(a,b) = a^2 + b^2. Then the total sum over T_n is something. For a vertical line x=i, the sum of p over points on it is sum_{y=1}^{n+1-i} (i^2 + y^2) = (n+1-i)i^2 + sum_{y=1}^{n+1-i} y^2 = (n+1-i)i^2 + (n+1-i)(n+2-i)(2n+3-i)/6. That's something. The total sum of squares over all points in T_5 is something like sum_{s=2}^{6} (s-1)*? Actually sum of squares over points: sum_{a,b} (a^2+b^2) = sum_{a,b} a^2 + sum_{a,b} b^2 = 2 sum_{a=1}^{n} a^2 * (number of b such that a+b≤n+1) = 2 sum_{a=1}^{n} a^2 * (n+1-a) = 2 sum_{a=1}^{n} a^2 (n+1-a). For n=5, that's 2*(1^2*5 + 2^2*4 + 3^2*3 + 4^2*2 + 5^2*1) = 2*(5+16+27+32+25)=2*105=210. So total sum of squares is 210. The possible differences: For points with a+b ≤ n+1, a,b≥1, b-a ranges from -(n-1) to n-1? Actually max b-a: when a=1,b=n gives b-a=n-1; min when a=n,b=1 gives b-a=1-n. So differences from -(n-1) to n-1. But there are 2n-1 possible differences. We have n lines, so we can cover at most n differences. But we need to cover all points, not just differences; each point must be on some line. For a point (a,b), the line y=x+(b-a) would contain it. So if we choose lines corresponding to n distinct differences, we cover points whose difference is one of those n. But there are many points with differences not covered. So we cannot cover all points with only n lines of slope 1. We could use lines of different slopes. Maybe we can design a set of n sunny lines that cover all points. Let's think.

We have n lines. Each line is a set of points. The points are in a triangular region. Possibly we can use lines that are "general" and each covers many points. But is it possible to have all lines sunny? The problem asks to determine all k such that there exist n lines satisfying conditions. So we need to find the set of possible k. Likely k can be any integer from 0 to n, but maybe some restrictions like k cannot be n? Or maybe k must be at most something? Let's test small n.

Take n=3. Then points: a,b positive, a+b ≤4. So points: (1,1), (1,2), (2,1), (1,3), (2,2), (3,1). That's 6 points. We need 3 lines covering all these points. k is number of sunny lines. We want to see which k possible.

We already have vertical lines x=1,2,3 covering all points. That gives k=0. Similarly horizontal y=1,2,3 gives k=0. Can we have k=1? That means exactly one sunny line, and two non-sunny lines. Non-sunny lines: horizontal, vertical, slope -1. So we need to choose 2 lines from these types, and 1 sunny line, such that all points are covered. Is that possible? Let's try to construct.

We need to cover all 6 points. Perhaps use vertical line x=1 and horizontal line y=1, and a sunny line that covers the remaining points? Points: (1,1) is on both vertical and horizontal, covered. (1,2) on vertical, covered. (2,1) on horizontal, covered. (1,3) on vertical, covered. (2,2) is not on vertical x=1 nor horizontal y=1. So need sunny line to cover (2,2). (3,1) on horizontal y=1, covered. So we need sunny line to cover (2,2) and also maybe (2,2) is the only uncovered point? Also check (2,2) is only point not on x=1 or y=1? Actually (2,2) is not on x=1 (x=2) nor y=1 (y=2). So yes. So we need a sunny line that passes through (2,2) and also possibly other points, but it's okay if it covers only that point. However we also need the line to be sunny: not horizontal, vertical, or slope -1. So we can choose line through (2,2) with slope 2, for example. That line will be sunny. But we also need to ensure that the lines are distinct. So we have lines: x=1, y=1, and y = 2x -2? Actually through (2,2) slope 2: y-2=2(x-2) => y=2x-2. That line is sunny. Does it cover any other points? (1,0) not in set, (3,4) not. So fine. So we have 3 lines covering all points. Exactly one sunny line. So k=1 possible.

Can we have k=2? That means two sunny lines and one non-sunny line. Non-sunny line could be vertical, horizontal, or slope -1. Let's try to cover all points with one vertical line and two sunny lines? Or maybe use a slope -1 line. Let's attempt.

We need to cover 6 points with 3 lines. If we use vertical line x=1, then points with x=1 are (1,1),(1,2),(1,3) covered. The remaining points: (2,1),(2,2),(3,1). We need two sunny lines to cover these three points. Could we cover all three with two lines? Possibly one line covers (2,1) and (3,1)? But (2,1) and (3,1) have same y=1, so a horizontal line y=1 would cover them, but that's non-sunny. We need sunny lines. So we cannot use y=1 because it's non-sunny. But we could use a line that passes through (2,1) and (2,2)? That would be vertical x=2, non-sunny. So not allowed. Could we use a line through (2,2) and (3,1)? That line has slope (1-2)/(3-2) = -1, so slope -1, which is non-sunny (parallel to x+y=0). So that line is not sunny. So we need two sunny lines covering these three points. Each sunny line can contain at most 2 of these points? Let's see possible lines through two of these points: (2,1)-(2,2) vertical, not sunny; (2,1)-(3,1) horizontal, not sunny; (2,2)-(3,1) slope -1, not sunny. So any line through two of these points is non-sunny. So to cover all three points with two sunny lines, we would need one sunny line to cover two points, but that would be non-sunny. So impossible. What if we use a different non-sunny line? Perhaps use a slope -1 line? For n=3, points: (1,1),(1,2),(2,1),(1,3),(2,2),(3,1). Consider using line y = -x + 2? That passes through (1,1) and (2,0) not in set; (1,1) is on it? Actually y=-x+2 gives (1,1) yes. Also (2,0) not. So covers (1,1). Another slope -1 line: y = -x + 3? passes through (1,2) and (2,1). So covers (1,2),(2,1). That's two points. Then we need two sunny lines to cover the remaining points: (1,3),(2,2),(3,1). But (3,1) is on y=-x+4? Not in set. So we need to cover these three with two sunny lines. Again, any line through two of these points: (1,3)-(2,2) slope -1, non-sunny; (1,3)-(3,1) slope (1-3)/(3-1) = -2/2 = -1? Actually (1,3) to (3,1): slope (1-3)/(3-1) = -2/2 = -1, non-sunny; (2,2)-(3,1) slope -1, non-sunny. So no sunny line can contain two of these points. So we need to cover three points with two sunny lines, each line can contain at most one point? But then we would have two lines covering at most two points, leaving one uncovered. So impossible. What if we use a horizontal line? y=1 covers (2,1),(3,1) and also (1,1) but (1,1) is also covered. Then remaining points: (1,2),(1,3),(2,2). Need two sunny lines to cover these three. Again, any line through two of them: (1,2)-(1,3) vertical, non-sunny; (1,2)-(2,2) horizontal, non-sunny; (1,3)-(2,2) slope (2-3)/(2-1) = -1, non-sunny. So no sunny line contains two of these. So impossible. So k=2 seems impossible for n=3.

What about k=3? All lines sunny. Can we cover 6 points with 3 sunny lines? Possibly. Let's try to find three sunny lines covering all points. We need each line to be sunny, so not horizontal, vertical, slope -1. We can try to use lines that are "general" and each covers many points. For n=3, points: (1,1),(1,2),(2,1),(1,3),(2,2),(3,1). Could we cover them with three lines? For instance, line through (1,1),(2,2),(3,3) but (3,3) not in set. So that line would be y=x, slope 1, sunny. It passes through (1,1) and (2,2). So covers two points. Another line through (1,2),(2,1)? That's slope -1, not sunny. So not allowed. Another line through (1,3),(2,2),(3,1)? That's slope -1, not sunny. So we need to cover (1,3),(2,2),(3,1) with sunny lines. Could we use line through (1,3) and (2,2) slope -1, not sunny. So not. Could we use line through (1,3) and (3,1) slope -1, not sunny. So any line through two of these points is non-sunny. So to cover all three points, we would need at least one sunny line that contains at least two of them, but that's impossible. So we need to cover them with two sunny lines, but each sunny line can contain at most one of these points? Then we would have two lines covering at most two points, leaving one uncovered. So k=3 impossible for n=3.

Thus for n=3, possible k: 0,1. So k=2,3 not possible.

Now test n=4. Points: a,b≥1, a+b ≤5. So points: s=2: (1,1); s=3: (1,2),(2,1); s=4: (1,3),(2,2),(3,1); s=5: (1,4),(2,3),(3,2),(4,1). Total points = 1+2+3+4=10. Need 4 lines covering all 10 points. k is number of sunny lines.

We already have vertical lines x=1,2,3,4 covering all points. So k=0 works. Similarly horizontal y=1,2,3,4 works. k=0.

Can we have k=1? Possibly use 3 non-sunny lines (vertical, horizontal, slope -1) and 1 sunny line to cover all points. But we need to check if we can cover all points with 3 non-sunny lines and 1 sunny line. For n=4, we have 10 points. If we take vertical lines x=1,2,3? That would be 3 lines covering points with x=1,2,3. That covers many points but not those with x=4: (4,1) is the only point with x=4? Actually points with x=4: (4,1) since a+b≤5, a=4,b=1 only. So vertical lines x=1,2,3 cover all points except (4,1). Then we need one sunny line to cover (4,1). That's fine, we can choose a sunny line through (4,1) (e.g., slope 2: y-1=2(x-4) => y=2x-7). That line is sunny. So we have 4 lines: x=1,x=2,x=3, and y=2x-7. But we need exactly k=1 sunny line, so that works. However we need to ensure that the lines are distinct. So k=1 possible.

Can we have k=2? That means 2 sunny lines and 2 non-sunny lines. We need to cover all 10 points with 2 non-sunny lines and 2 sunny lines. Non-sunny lines: could be vertical, horizontal, slope -1. Let's see if we can cover all points with 2 vertical lines? That would cover points with x=1 and x=2, but then points with x=3,4 remain. That's 3+4? Actually points with x=3: (3,1),(3,2) (since a=3,b≤2) so 2 points; x=4: (4,1) only. So total 3 points uncovered. We need to cover them with 2 sunny lines. Could we cover these 3 points with 2 sunny lines? Possibly one sunny line covers (3,1) and (4,1)? That would be horizontal y=1, but that's non-sunny. So not allowed. Could we cover (3,1) and (3,2) with a vertical line x=3, non-sunny. So not. Could we cover (3,1) and (4,1) with a line of slope? That line would be y = 1? Actually (3,1) and (4,1) have same y, so horizontal y=1, non-sunny. So no sunny line can contain two of these points because any line through two of these points is either vertical (x=3) or horizontal (y=1) or slope -1? (3,1) and (4,1) horizontal; (3,1) and (3,2) vertical; (3,2) and (4,1) slope (1-2)/(4-3) = -1, non-sunny. So any line through two of these points is non-sunny. So to cover three points with two sunny lines, each sunny line can contain at most one of these points, so at most two points covered, leaving one uncovered. So impossible. What if we use a different pair of non-sunny lines? Perhaps use one vertical and one horizontal? For example, take vertical x=1 and horizontal y=1. Then points covered: x=1: (1,1),(1,2),(1,3),(1,4); y=1: (1,1),(2,1),(3,1),(4,1). So covered points: all with x=1 or y=1. That leaves points: (2,2),(2,3),(3,2),(4,1)? Actually (4,1) is on y=1, covered. So uncovered: (2,2),(2,3),(3,2). Also (3,1) on y=1, covered; (1,2) on x=1, covered; etc. So three points uncovered: (2,2),(2,3),(3,2). Now we need to cover these three with two sunny lines. So cannot cover all three with two sunny lines. Could we use a different combination? Maybe use two slope -1 lines? For n=4, points: (1,1),(1,2),(2,1),(1,3),(2,2),(3,1),(1,4),(2,3),(3,2),(4,1). Consider using lines of slope -1: y = -x + c. For c=2: passes through (1,1) and (2,0) not; only (1,1) maybe? Actually (1,1) satisfies y=-x+2? 1=-1+2=1 yes. So covers (1,1). For c=3: passes through (1,2),(2,1). So covers (1,2),(2,1). For c=4: passes through (1,3),(2,2),(3,1). So covers (1,3),(2,2),(3,1). For c=5: passes through (1,4),(2,3),(3,2),(4,1). So covers (1,4),(2,3),(3,2),(4,1). So we can cover all points with 4 slope -1 lines? Actually we have 4 lines: c=2,3,4,5. But they are all slope -1, so non-sunny. That's k=0. For k=2, we want two sunny lines and two non-sunny lines. Could we use two slope -1 lines covering many points, and two sunny lines covering the rest? For instance, use c=4 and c=5? So we need to cover four points with two sunny lines; each sunny line can contain at most 2 of these points? But we saw only one sunny line can contain two of them (the slope 1 line). The other two points cannot be together on a sunny line. So we would need one sunny line to cover (1,3) alone and another sunny line to cover (3,1) alone. What about using c=2 and c=4? Then c=2 covers (1,1); c=4 covers (1,3),(2,2),(3,1). That's 1+3=4 points. Remaining: s=3: (1,2),(2,1); s=5: (1,4),(2,3),(3,2),(4,1) that's 2+4=6 points. Need two sunny lines to cover 6 points. Possibly each sunny line covers at most 2 points? But we need to cover 6 points with two lines, so each must cover at least 3 points. Can a sunny line cover 3 points among these? Points: (1,2),(2,1),(1,4),(2,3),(3,2),(4,1). Check if any line can contain three of them. For a line to contain three lattice points, they must be collinear. Could there be a line through (1,2),(2,1),(3,0) not in set; (1,2),(2,3),(3,4) maybe? (1,2) to (2,3) slope1, line y=x+1. Does it contain (3,4)? 3+1=4 yes, but (3,4) not in set. (1,4),(2,3),(3,2) slope -1, non-sunny. (1,4),(2,3),(3,2) is slope -1, not sunny. (2,1),(3,2),(4,3) slope1, but (4,3) not in set. (1,2),(3,2) horizontal y=2, non-sunny. (2,1),(4,1) horizontal y=1, non-sunny. So no sunny line contains three of these points. So each sunny line can cover at most 2 points. Then two lines cover at most 4 points, leaving at least 2 uncovered. So impossible.

Thus for n=4, k=2 seems impossible. But we haven't exhausted all possibilities for non-sunny lines: they could be a mix of vertical, horizontal, slope -1. Also we could have more than two non-sunny lines? For k=2, we have exactly 2 sunny lines and 2 non-sunny lines. So we need to choose 2 non-sunny lines (could be both vertical, both horizontal, both slope -1, or one vertical and one horizontal, etc.) and 2 sunny lines covering all points. Let's try to see if there is any configuration that works.

We can try to use a vertical line x=1 and a horizontal line y=1. Then covered points: all with x=1 or y=1. That's 4+4-1=7 points (since (1,1) counted twice). The uncovered points: those with x≥2 and y≥2: (2,2),(2,3),(3,2),(4,1)? Actually (4,1) has y=1 so covered; (3,1) covered; (2,1) covered; (1,4) covered; (1,3) covered; (1,2) covered; (1,1) covered. So uncovered: (2,2),(2,3),(3,2). Also (3,1) covered; (4,1) covered; (3,2) is uncovered; (2,2) uncovered; (2,3) uncovered. That's 3 points. Need to cover these with 2 sunny lines. As before, any line through two of these points is non-sunny. So each sunny line can cover at most one of these points, so at most 2 covered, leaving one uncovered. So impossible.

What about using one vertical and one slope -1? For instance, take vertical x=1 and slope -1 line y=-x+5 (c=5). Then covered points: x=1: (1,1),(1,2),(1,3),(1,4); y=-x+5: (1,4),(2,3),(3,2),(4,1). So covered: (1,1),(1,2),(1,3),(1,4),(2,3),(3,2),(4,1). That's 7 points. Uncovered: (2,1),(2,2),(3,1). Actually (2,1) is not covered? Check: x=2,y=1 is not on x=1 nor y=-x+5? For x=2, y=-2+5=3, so (2,1) not. (2,2) not; (3,1) not. So 3 points left. Need to cover these with 2 sunny lines. Points: (2,1),(2,2),(3,1). Check lines: (2,1)-(2,2) vertical x=2, non-sunny; (2,1)-(3,1) horizontal y=1, non-sunny; (2,2)-(3,1) slope (1-2)/(3-2)=-1, non-sunny. So again no sunny pair. So impossible.

What about using two slope -1 lines? Already considered c=4 and c=5 gave uncovered 3 points; c=3 and c=5 gave uncovered 5 points; c=2 and c=5 gave uncovered 5 points; c=2 and c=4 gave uncovered 6 points; c=3 and c=4 gave uncovered 5 points; c=2 and c=3 gave uncovered 4 points? Actually c=2 covers (1,1); c=3 covers (1,2),(2,1); that's 3 points. Uncovered: s=4,5: (1,3),(2,2),(3,1),(1,4),(2,3),(3,2),(4,1) that's 7 points. Need two sunny lines to cover 7 points. Possibly each sunny line can cover many points? But we need to see if any sunny line can contain more than 2 points among these. Points: (1,3),(2,2),(3,1),(1,4),(2,3),(3,2),(4,1). Check if any line can contain three of them. For instance, line through (1,3),(2,2),(3,1) slope -1, non-sunny. (1,4),(2,3),(3,2) slope -1, non-sunny. (2,2),(3,2) horizontal, non-sunny. (1,3),(1,4) vertical, non-sunny. (2,3),(3,2) slope -1. (3,1),(4,1) horizontal. So no sunny line with three points. So each sunny line covers at most 2 points. Two lines cover at most 4 points, leaving at least 3 uncovered. So impossible.

Thus k=2 seems impossible for n=4.

What about k=3? That would be 3 sunny lines and 1 non-sunny line. Can we cover all 10 points with 1 non-sunny line and 3 sunny lines? Possibly. Let's try to see if we can find such a configuration.

We need to cover all points. The non-sunny line could be vertical, horizontal, or slope -1. Suppose we take vertical line x=1. Then points with x=1 are covered: (1,1),(1,2),(1,3),(1,4). So we have some sunny pairs: (2,1)-(3,2), (2,2)-(4,1), (2,3)-(3,1). That's three disjoint pairs? So conjecture: k=2 is impossible for all n≥3. But we need to check n=3,4,5,6. So far yes. Could there be a configuration with k=2 for larger n? Possibly if the sunny lines cover many points, and the non-sunny lines cover the rest. But we need to cover all points. The points are in a triangular array. The maximum number of points on a sunny line is roughly n? Actually for slope 1, the line y=x passes through points (1,1),(2,2),...,(n,n) but (n,n) has sum 2n, which is > n+1 for n≥3? Actually a+b ≤ n+1, so for n≥3, n+n = 2n > n+1, so the only points on y=x that satisfy a+b ≤ n+1 are those with a+b ≤ n+1, i.e., a ≤ (n+1)/2. So the number of points on y=x in the triangle is floor((n+1)/2). For n=5, that's 3; for n=6, that's 3 (since (n+1)/2=3.5, floor=3). So maximum is about n/2. So each sunny line can cover at most about n/2 points. To cover all N = n(n+1)/2 points with n lines, we need each line to cover on average (n+1)/2 points. So if we have only 2 sunny lines, they can cover at most about n points each (since each at most ~n/2). So total points covered by sunny lines ≤ n. The remaining points (N - n) must be covered by non-sunny lines. But non-sunny lines are of types that can cover many points: e.g., a vertical line x=i covers points with that x, which is about n-i+1 points; a horizontal line y=j covers about n-j+1; a slope -1 line y=-x+c covers points with sum=c, which is about c-1 points. So if we have n-k non-sunny lines, they can cover at most the sum of the largest n-k such lines. But we need to cover N - (points covered by sunny lines) points. For k=2, we have n-2 non-sunny lines. The maximum total points they can cover is when we choose the n-2 lines that cover the most points. But we also need to ensure that the sunny lines cover points that are not covered by non-sunny lines, otherwise we might have overlap. However, we can choose the lines to minimize overlap. So we need to see if there exists a set of n lines (2 sunny, n-2 non-sunny) covering all points. This is a covering problem.

We can try to prove that k=2 is impossible. Perhaps we can use a counting argument: each point (a,b) is on at least one line. The lines are distinct. For each line, we can consider the number of points it contains. We need to sum over lines of the number of points on them, but points can be on multiple lines, so the sum is at least the number of points. We can try to get an upper bound on the sum of points per line given the types of lines. For sunny lines, the maximum number of points they can contain is limited. For non-sunny lines, they can contain many points, but we have only n-2 of them. Maybe we can show that the sum of points on n-2 non-sunny lines is at most something less than N minus the maximum possible points on 2 sunny lines. But we need to consider that the sunny lines might also cover points that are on non-sunny lines, so the sum of points on lines (counting multiplicities) is at least N. If we can show that for any configuration with 2 sunny lines, the sum of points on lines is less than N, then it's impossible. But we need to consider the worst-case for the sum. Actually we want to prove that no configuration exists. So we need to show that for any set of n lines with exactly 2 sunny lines, the total number of distinct points covered is less than N. But we can choose the lines to maximize coverage. So we need to find the maximum possible number of distinct points covered by n lines with exactly 2 sunny lines. If that maximum is less than N, then k=2 impossible. So we need to find the maximum coverage.

Alternatively, we can try to characterize which k are possible. From small n, we have k=0,1,3 possible for n≥4; for n=3, k=0,1. So maybe for n≥4, k can be 0,1,3? But what about k=n? For n=4, k=4 impossible; for n=5, k=5 impossible; for n=6, likely impossible. So maybe k can be 0,1,3 only. But we need to check if k=2 is possible for some larger n? Let's test n=7 quickly with a possible construction. For n=7, points: sum 2..8, total points = 1+2+3+4+5+6+7=28. Need 7 lines. Could we have 2 sunny lines? Possibly we can use the line y=x to cover (1,1),(2,2),(3,3) — 3 points; and another sunny line like y=2x-1? That passes through (1,1) already, (2,3) and (3,5) but (3,5) sum8, yes; so (2,3) and (3,5) are on it; that's 2 points. So total sunny points = 3+2=5? But we can choose better sunny lines. For n=7, the maximum points on a sunny line? For slope 1, we have points (1,1),(2,2),(3,3) — 3 points; (4,4) sum8>8? Actually 4+4=8, sum≤8? For n=7, a+b≤8, so (4,4) sum8 is allowed. So (4,4) is in the triangle. So y=x actually contains (1,1),(2,2),(3,3),(4,4) — 4 points! Because sum=2,4,6,8 all ≤8. So for n=7, the line y=x contains points with a=b and a+b≤8, so a≤4. So 4 points. Similarly, line y=2x? Through (1,2) and (2,4) and (3,6) sum9>8, so only 2 points. So the maximum is 4. So we could have a sunny line covering 4 points. Another sunny line could cover 4 points? Possibly y=x+? For slope 1, the line y=x+c? For c=0, we have 4 points; for c=1, y=x+1 gives points (1,2),(2,3),(3,4),(4,5) but (4,5) sum9>8, so only 3 points; for c=-1, y=x-1 gives (2,1),(3,2),(4,3) — 3 points. So max is 4. So we could have 2 sunny lines each covering 4 points, total 8 points. Then we need 5 non-sunny lines to cover the remaining 20 points. Is that possible? The non-sunny lines can be of types: vertical, horizontal, slope -1. For n=7, the sums range 2..8. The slope -1 lines can cover many points: e.g., c=2 covers 1 point; c=3 covers 2; c=4 covers 3; c=5 covers 4; c=6 covers 5; c=7 covers 6; c=8 covers 7. So if we take 5 slope -1 lines, we can cover at most the sum of the largest 5 sums: 4+5+6+7+8? Actually sums 4..8: 3+4+5+6+7=25 points, but that's counting all points in those sums, but we only need to cover 20 points. However, we also have the sunny lines covering 8 points, but those points might be among these 25, so we need to ensure that the total distinct points covered is 28. If the sunny lines cover points that are not in the non-sunny lines, then we need the non-sunny lines to cover the rest. But if we choose the non-sunny lines to cover sums 4..8, that's 25 points, but we only need to cover 20 points, so we could choose a subset. However, the sunny lines might also cover some points in those sums, so we need to ensure that the union is all 28 points. The points in sums 2 and 3 are: s=2: (1,1); s=3: (1,2),(2,1) — 3 points. Those are not covered by slope -1 lines if we take c=4..8. So we need to cover those 3 points with the sunny lines. But we have 2 sunny lines covering 4 points each, so they could cover those 3 points plus one more. For instance, take sunny line y=x (covers (1,1),(2,2),(3,3),(4,4) — that's 4 points, but (1,1) is in s=2, (2,2) in s=4, (3,3) in s=6, (4,4) in s=8. So it covers (1,1) and also (2,2),(3,3),(4,4). Then we need to cover (1,2) and (2,1) from s=3. Could we cover them with another sunny line? For instance, line y=2x-1? That passes through (1,1) already, (2,3) and (3,5) but (2,1) not. So not. Maybe line y= -2x+? Through (1,2) and (2,0) no. So we need a sunny line that contains (1,2) and (2,1) but that's slope -1, non-sunny. So we cannot cover both with one sunny line. Could we cover (1,2) with a sunny line that also covers some other points, and (2,1) with a different sunny line? But we only have 2 sunny lines. So we need to cover these 2 points with the sunny lines, but we already used y=x to cover (1,1). So we have one sunny line left. That one sunny line can contain at most 4 points, but can it contain both (1,2) and (2,1)? No, because that line is slope -1. So it can contain at most one of them. So we would need at least 3 sunny lines to cover s=2 and s=3 points. So k=2 impossible for n=7.

Thus pattern suggests that k=2 is impossible for all n≥3. But we need a rigorous proof.

Now, what about k=0,1,3? We have constructions for n=3: k=0,1; n=4: k=0,1,3; n=5: k=0,1,3; n=6: we haven't constructed k=3 yet, but we can likely extend the pattern. For n=6, we can try to find a configuration with k=3. We already have a construction for n=5 with k=3. For n=6, we can try to use similar idea: take two vertical lines x=1 and x=2, and then three sunny lines covering the rest. For n=6, points: sum 2..7. Uncovered: points with x≥3: (3,3),(3,4),(4,2),(4,3),(5,2),(6,1) — that's 6 points? Maybe use 2 horizontal lines and 1 vertical? Similar.

Thus for n=6, we have k=0,1,3 possible. So pattern: for n≥4, k can be 0,1,3. But what about k=n? For n=4, k=4 impossible; for n=5, k=5 impossible; for n=6, k=6? We can try to see if k=6 possible. For n=6, we need 6 sunny lines covering 21 points. Each sunny line can cover at most 3 points (since y=x covers 3 points; other slopes cover at most 2). So 6 lines cover at most 18 points, but we need 21. So impossible. So k=n is impossible for n≥4? For n=3, k=3 impossible as we saw. So k=n never possible? For n=3, we had k=0,1 only. So k=n not possible.

Now, what about k=2? We have not found any construction. Could k=2 be possible for some n? Let's try n=4 we already saw k=2 impossible. n=5 impossible. n=6 impossible. n=7? Possibly if we can design a configuration with 2 sunny lines covering many points, and the other 5 non-sunny lines covering the rest. For n=7, points: sum 2..8, total points = 1+2+3+4+5+6+7=28. Sunny lines max points? For slope 1, y=x covers points with a=b and sum≤8, so a≤4, that's 4 points. Another sunny line could cover 4 points? Possibly y=x+? So each non-sunny line can contain at most n points. But we have n-2 non-sunny lines, so the maximum total points (counting multiplicities) from non-sunny lines is at most n(n-2). The sunny lines can contain at most 2 * floor((n+1)/2) points. So the sum of points on all lines (counting multiplicities) is at most n(n-2) + 2 floor((n+1)/2). But we need this sum to be at least N = n(n+1)/2. So we need n(n-2) + 2 floor((n+1)/2) ≥ n(n+1)/2. But we need to consider that points can be on multiple lines, so the sum of points per line is an overcount. The condition that each point is on at least one line means the sum of points per line is at least N. So we need to show that for any configuration, the sum of points per line is ≤ something less than N? Actually we want to show that it's impossible to have sum ≥ N. But the sum can be larger than N due to overlaps. So we need a different approach.

Maybe we can use a weighting argument: assign to each point a weight such that the total weight of all points is something, and each line has total weight at most something, and the sum over lines is at most something, leading to a contradiction. This is common in covering problems.

Perhaps we can use a combinatorial argument based on the fact that the points are in a triangular array, and the lines are of specific types. Another idea: Consider the set of points with a+b = n+1 (the boundary). There are n points. But we can also consider the interior points. Because if a line contains two boundary points, then the slope would be (b2-b1)/(a2-a1). For two points on the boundary, their sums are n+1, so they are of the form (a, n+1-a) and (b, n+1-b). Another idea: Use the concept of "covering number" of a triangular grid by lines. Let's try to prove by contradiction. Suppose we have n lines covering T_n, with exactly 2 sunny lines L1 and L2. Consider the points on the boundary B = {(a,b)∈T_n : a+b=n+1}. There are n points. There are n-2 non-sunny lines. So each non-sunny line must cover at least one point of B, and since there are exactly n-2 non-sunny lines and n-2 boundary points left, each non-sunny line must cover exactly one boundary point, and each boundary point is covered by exactly one non-sunny line. So the non-sunny lines are such that each covers exactly one boundary point, and no non-sunny line covers two boundary points. Therefore, none of the non-sunny lines is a slope -1 line with c=n+1 (since that would cover all n boundary points). Also, they cannot be slope -1 lines with c≠n+1 because those cover 0 boundary points. So the non-sunny lines are either horizontal or vertical lines. Because a horizontal line y=j covers exactly one boundary point (n+1-j, j). A vertical line x=i covers exactly one boundary point (i, n+1-i). So the n-2 non-sunny lines are all either horizontal or vertical. And they cover all boundary points except the two covered by L1 and L2. So the set of boundary points covered by non-sunny lines is exactly B \ {p,q} where p and q are the boundary points on L1 and L2. So we have n-2 horizontal/vertical lines that together cover all boundary points except p and q.

Now, consider the interior points. The interior points are those with sum ≤ n. There are n(n-1)/2 such points. Now, we need to prove this in general. The key is to show that the number of points not on the horizontal/vertical lines (the "complement") is at least something that cannot be covered by 2 sunny lines. Perhaps we can show that the complement contains at least 3 points that are not collinear on any sunny line? But we need a bound.

Let's try to analyze the structure of the complement. We have n-2 horizontal/vertical lines that cover all boundary points except p and q. Let the set of vertical lines be V = {v_1,...,v_a} and horizontal lines H = {h_1,...,h_b}, with a+b = n-2. The boundary points are (i, n+1-i) for i=1..n. The condition that each boundary point is either on a vertical line or a horizontal line (since the non-sunny lines cover all but p,q, and p,q are on sunny lines). So for each i, either i ∈ V or n+1-i ∈ H, except for the two i corresponding to p and q. Now, we need to produce a rigorous solution.

Let's write the solution step by step.

First, define T_n = {(a,b)∈ℕ^2 : a,b≥1, a+b≤n+1}. There are N = n(n+1)/2 points.

We need to find all k such that there exist n distinct lines covering T_n, with exactly k sunny lines.

We'll prove:
- k=0 is always possible.
- k=1 is always possible.
- k=3 is possible for n≥4.
- k=2 is impossible for all n≥3.
- For n=3, k=0,1 are possible; for n≥4, k=0,1,3 are possible.

We need to verify that k=3 is possible for n=4 as well. The construction for n=4: take vertical lines x=1,2 (2 lines) and then three sunny lines: e.g., y=2x-5, y=2x-4, y=2x-7. That covers all points. So k=3 works.

Now, we need to show that k=2 is impossible. We'll do a case analysis.

Assume there exists a set of n lines covering T_n with exactly 2 sunny lines. Let the sunny lines be L1 and L2. Consider the boundary B = {(a,b)∈T_n : a+b=n+1}. There are n points. Each line that is not sunny is either horizontal, vertical, or slope -1. If a line is slope -1, it contains all points with a+b=c for some c. If c=n+1, it contains all boundary points. So we have two cases.

Case 1: There is a non-sunny line ℓ with equation y=-x+(n+1). Then ℓ covers all boundary points. The remaining points are those with a+b≤n, i.e., T_{n-1}. We need to cover T_{n-1} with the other n-2 lines (2 sunny and n-3 non-sunny). But it is known that the minimum number of lines needed to cover T_{n-1} is n-1 (since each line can contain at most n-1 points, and T_{n-1} has (n-1)n/2 points). More rigorously: For any line ℓ, the number of points of T_{n-1} on ℓ is at most n-1 (if ℓ is vertical x=1, it contains n-1 points; if ℓ is slope -1, it contains at most n-1 points; if ℓ is other slope, it contains at most floor((n-1)/2) points). But we need a lower bound on the number of lines required. Since T_{n-1} has (n-1)n/2 points, and each line can cover at most n-1 points, we need at least n lines? Wait, (n-1)n/2 divided by (n-1) = n/2, so that's not a good bound. Actually we need to show that the covering number is at least n-1. The known result: the minimum number of lines needed to cover all points of a triangular array of side m is m. For m=n-1, that's n-1. So we need at least n-1 lines. But we have only n-2 lines. So impossible. This is a standard fact; we can prove it by considering that each line can contain at most 2 points from the set of points with a+b = n (the next layer)? Actually we can give a direct proof: In T_{n-1}, consider the points with a+b = n. There are n-1 such points. Any line that is not slope -1 can contain at most 1 of them? Let's analyze: A line with slope m≠-1 can contain at most 1 point from the set of points with a+b = n? For a line with slope -1, it contains all points with a+b = constant, so it can contain at most 1 such set (if c=n, it contains all n-1 points). So if we have a slope -1 line with c=n, it covers all n-1 points of that layer. So we could cover that layer with one line. Then the next layer a+b = n-1 has n-2 points, etc. So the covering number for T_{n-1} is n-1 if we use slope -1 lines? Actually we can cover T_{n-1} with n-1 slope -1 lines: c=3,...,n+1? But T_{n-1} has sums from 2 to n. So we need c=2,...,n. That's n-1 lines. So the covering number is n-1. So we need at least n-1 lines. Since we have n-2 lines, impossible. So case 1 is impossible.

Case 2: There is no non-sunny line that covers all boundary points. Then each non-sunny line can cover at most 1 boundary point (since horizontal and vertical cover exactly 1, and slope -1 with c≠n+1 covers 0). There are n-2 non-sunny lines, so they can cover at most n-2 boundary points. Since the two sunny lines can cover at most 2 boundary points (each can cover at most 1), the total boundary points covered is at most n, which is exactly n. For a covering, we need all n boundary points to be covered. Therefore, the non-sunny lines must cover exactly n-2 boundary points, and the sunny lines must cover the remaining 2 boundary points. So we have a bijection: each boundary point is either on a sunny line or on a non-sunny line. The two sunny lines cover two distinct boundary points, say B1=(i1, n+1-i1) and B2=(i2, n+1-i2). The other n-2 boundary points are covered by the non-sunny lines. Since each non-sunny line covers exactly one boundary point, they are all either horizontal or vertical. Let V be the set of x-coordinates of vertical non-sunny lines, and H be the set of y-coordinates of horizontal non-sunny lines. Then for each i not in {i1,i2}, we have i∈V or n+1-i∈H. Now, define X = {1,...,n}\V and Y = {1,...,n}\H. Then as argued, for any x∈X, we have x∉V, so the condition forces n+1-x∈H, i.e., n+1-x∉Y. Thus Y∩(n+1-X)=∅. Similarly, X∩(n+1-Y)=∅. The map i→n+1-i is a bijection, so |n+1-X|=|X|, |n+1-Y|=|Y|. Hence |Y| ≤ n - |X| and |X| ≤ n - |Y|. Therefore, |X|+|Y| ≤ n. On the other hand, the number of non-sunny lines is |V|+|H| = n-2. But |V| = n - |X|, |H| = n - |Y|, so (n - |X|)+(n - |Y|)= n-2 ⇒ 2n - (|X|+|Y|)= n-2 ⇒ |X|+|Y| = n+2. This contradicts |X|+|Y| ≤ n. Hence case 2 is impossible.

Thus k=2 is impossible for all n≥3.

Now, we need to show that k=0,1,3 are possible for n≥4. For n=3, we have k=0,1 possible. We'll give explicit constructions.

- For k=0: take lines x=1, x=2, ..., x=n. These are all vertical, hence not sunny. They cover all points because for any (a,b)∈T_n, a∈{1,...,n}, so it lies on line x=a.
- For k=1: take lines x=1, x=2, ..., x=n-1 (these are n-1 vertical lines, not sunny) and one line through the remaining point (n,1) with slope 2, e.g., y-1=2(x-n). This line is sunny because slope 2 ≠0,∞,-1. It covers (n,1) and possibly other points, but that's fine. The lines are distinct. So we have n lines with exactly 1 sunny line.
- For k=3: we need to construct for n≥4. For n=4, take lines x=1, x=2 (2 vertical lines) and three sunny lines: e.g., L1: y=2x-5 (through (3,1)), L2: y=2x-4 (through (3,2)), L3: y=2x-7 (through (4,1)). Check coverage: x=1 covers (1,1)-(1,4); x=2 covers (2,1)-(2,3); L1 covers (3,1); L2 covers (3,2); L3 covers (4,1). All points covered. So k=3 works for n=4. For n≥5, take vertical lines x=1,2,...,n-3 (these are n-3 lines, not sunny). Then the uncovered points are those with x≥n-2. There are 6 such points: (n-2,1),(n-2,2),(n-2,3),(n-1,1),(n-1,2),(n,1). Define three sunny lines:
   L1: y = x - (n-3) (passes through (n-2,1) and (n-1,2))
   L2: y = -x/2 + (n+2)/2 (passes through (n-2,2) and (n,1))
   L3: y = -2x + (2n-1) (passes through (n-2,3) and (n-1,1))
These lines are sunny because slopes are 1, -1/2, -2, none of which are 0,∞,-1. They cover all 6 points. So total lines: n-3 vertical + 3 sunny = n lines, exactly 3 sunny. Thus k=3 is possible for all n≥4.

Now, we also need to check if k=2 is possible for n=3? We already know it's impossible. So the set of possible k for n=3 is {0,1}. For n≥4, it's {0,1,3}.

But we should also consider if k=0,1,3 are possible for n=3? For n=3, k=3 is impossible (we already saw). So answer: For n=3, k=0,1; for n≥4, k=0,1,3.

Thus the solution is complete.

This is a common technique.

Let's try to find a weighting that makes each line have total weight at most 1, and the total weight of all points is >1. Now, consider a covering of T_n by n lines with exactly 2 sunny lines. We have two cases.

Case 1: There is a non-sunny line ℓ with equation y=-x+(n+1). Then ℓ covers all boundary points. The remaining lines are 2 sunny lines L1, L2 and n-3 other non-sunny lines. They must cover T_{n-1}. We need a more precise counting. Let's consider the layers from top to bottom. Let L_s be the set of points with a+b=s. There are s-1 points. If we use a slope -1 line for each layer, we would need n slope -1 lines (since there are n layers). But we have at most n-2 slope -1 lines. Therefore, at least 2 layers must be covered by other lines. Those layers have many points. For a layer s, if we do not have a slope -1 line with constant s, then each point in that layer must be covered by a line that is not slope -1. Since each such line can cover at most 1 point from that layer, we need at least (s-1) lines to cover that layer. But we have only n lines total. So the layers that are not covered by slope -1 lines must be small. For example, if we have a layer s with s-1 points, and we cover it by non-slope -1 lines, we need at least s-1 lines. Since we have only n lines, this forces s-1 ≤ n. That's always true for s≤n+1. But we also have to cover other layers. So the total number of lines needed to cover all layers if we use slope -1 lines for some layers and individual points for others is at least the sum over layers of (number of points in layer) divided by (max points per line for that layer). But we have a limited number of lines.

This follows from the fact that the total H on any set of n lines that cover T_n is at most S, and equality can only be achieved when the lines are a partition of the points (i.e., each point appears exactly once). But we have a covering with sunny lines, which are not a partition because they overlap? Actually they could overlap, but that would increase the total H. For example, if two sunny lines both contain the same point, the total H would be larger than S. So it's possible that a covering with sunny lines has total H > S. So we need to show that any covering with exactly 2 sunny lines must have total H < S. This is not automatically true; we need to argue that the sunny lines cannot have enough points to compensate for the loss from replacing slope -1 lines. Let's compute the maximum total H from n lines that cover T_n. The total H is the sum over points of (a+b) times the number of lines containing that point. To maximize this, we would want to have many lines containing the points with large a+b. The points with largest a+b are the boundary points (sum=n+1). There are n such points. The best way to cover them is to have a line that contains all n boundary points, i.e., the slope -1 line with c=n+1. That line gives a contribution of n(n+1) to the total H. Then we need to cover the interior points. The next largest a+b are points with sum=n. There are n-1 such points. To cover them, we can use a slope -1 line with c=n, which gives H = n(n-1). That's a large contribution. So we can use slope -1 lines for all layers. That gives total H = sum_{c=2}^{n+1} c(c-1) = S. So that's a covering with total H=S. Could we get a larger total H by using some lines that are not slope -1 but that contain many points? For example, a vertical line x=1 contains points with a+b from 2 to n+1. Its H is n(n+3)/2.
